APPENDIX I
HOW TO DETERMINE EKĀDAŚĪ  BASIC TERMS AND GENERAL RULES  When thinking of days and months, we generally think of a solar calendar, based upon the movement of the sun within the  zodiac. The time taken by the sun to traverse the whole zodiac  is a little more than 365 days, and this is usually divided into  twelve solar months and 5a weeks. The dates of all festivals  mentioned in the Hari-bhakti-vilāsa are, however, calculated  according to a lunar calendar. This makes the determination  of festival dates a little complicated.
While the solar calendar is based on the movement of the  sun, the lunar calendar is based on the movement of the moon  in relation to the sun. The lunar calendar consists of months,  the time between a full moon (when the whole moon as seen  from the earth is illuminated) and the next. This takes about  29.5 days. Twelve such months make up a lunar year. The lunar  year will thus have about 354 days, eleven less than the solar  year. This discrepancy means that the position of a date of the  lunar calender ín the solar calendar will change from year to  year. In order to synchronize the two calendars, an extra month  (adhika-māsa) is added to the lunar calendar approximately  every third year. In India both calendars have been used since  ancient times.
In India a day is the time between one sunrise and the next.  This means that a day of fasting will generally begin at sunrise  and continue to the next sunrise, not from midnight to midnight as we would think in the west. This is quite simple. More  difficult to understand is the lunar day, tithi. A tithi has nothing to do with the rising and setting of the sun, but instead  with the different phases of the moon. The first tithi of a lunar  month starts when the moon is full, or when the angle be
tween the sun and moon is 18o degrees. It continues until the  angle between sun and moon has increased with 12 degrees.  Then the second tithi starts, and it continues until 12 more degrees have passed. When 15 such tithis have passed, the angle  between sun and moon is o degrees, which means that the moon  can not be seen at all. That is called new moon. Then the angle  will gradually decrease during the next 15 tithis, until the  moon again becomes full and a lunar month is completed.  The angular speed of the moon in relation to the sun is not always the same, so the length of one tithi can vary between 19  and 26 hours. Because of this, a tithi can start at any time of  the day.
If fasting is enjoined on, for example, the eighth tithi, one  must first determine the exact time when the tithi begins. For  this one must observe the moon, or more practically use a table  of someone else's observations and calculations. If the tithi continues over more than one day, the day whose sunrise falls  within the tithi should be chosen (see figure 1). If more than  one sunrise occurs within the tithi, preference is given to the  first. If no sunrise is touched, the day in which the tithi falls is  chosen (see figure 2). The fasting should be observed on the  day thus determined.
The 15 tithis when the moon is waning is called the krsnapaksa or dark fortnight, and the 15 tithis when the moon is  waxing is called the śukla-paksa or light fortnight. In both fortnights, except for the Full Moon and New Moon, the tithis are  named after their number. The ninth tithi is thus called Navamī, the tenth Daśamī, the eleventh Ekādaśī, the twelfth Dvādaśī and the thirteenth Trayodaśī. These tithis are the ones  mentioned in this book.
Two more terms must be understood before proceeding.  The background belt of stars in the sky within which the planets move is called the zodiac. In both in India and the west the  zodiac is divided into twelve astrological signs. The names of  the signs are almost the same in India as in the west. The Indian zodiac is further divided into 27 lunar mansions or aster
isms, each covering 13 degrees and 20 minutes of the zodiac.  When the moon is situated in one of these asterisms, the  present tithi is said to be ćombined' with that asterism.




WHEN TO CELEBRATE EKADAŚI
As already noted, Ekādaśī means eleventh: There will be two  eleventh tithis in a month, one during the dark fortnight and  one during the bright. One should always fast on both. Some  people differentiate between them and say that householders  should fast only during the bright fortnight, but the author  thinks such statements are meant for materialists and not for  Vaisnavas. Some authorities also think that one should not fast  in the case of sa¥krānti, when the sun enters a new sign of the  zodiac, or if there is a birth or death in the family. The author  refutes all these opinions. One should always fast on Ekādaśī.
However, there are tithis of two kinds: pure and mixed.'Pure'  means that it begins before sunrise of the day when one is to  celebrate it. For example, if Navamī tithi begins two hours before sunrise on August 8th, then August 8th is pure (śuddha)  Navamī. Had it begun even one minute after sunrise, it would  have been mixed with the previous tithi. If it continues beyond  the next sunrise, it is also mixed, but not impure. Additionally,  for Ekādaśī not to be mixed with the previous tithi, Daśamī, it  must begin at least two muhūrtas (1 hour 36 minutes) before  sunrise. If Ekādaśī is mixed with the previous tithi by beginning even a second too late, one should not fast on that day.  Instead one should fast on the next day, Dvādaśī. (see figure 2)  To fast on such an impure Ekādaśī is an offence to the Lord  and very detrimental.
Some authorities say that in order to be pure, Ekādaśī must  actually begin before midnight on the previous day, but that  view is rejected by the author. However, if Ekādaśī begins after  midnight, the first twelve hours of Ekādaśī cannot be used for  bathing, declaring the vrata or worship. For example, if Ekādaśī  begins at or.35, one can not take his morning bath until 13.35.  In this case the mandatory daily worship will naturally continue into the late evening.
To complete Ekādaśī, one should break the fast on the next  day after sunrise, after one has performed the mandatory ritu
als such as bathing and worshipping the Lord. The best time is  at midday. However, one must not let Dvādaśī pass before  breaking the fast. Different options for such a situation is given  in the text (13.238-256). One should also not break the fast during the first quarter of Dvādaśī, which is called Hari-vāsara.
MAHA-DVADAŚI  Now, even if Ekādaśī is pure, it should be rejected if the next  day is Mahā-dvādaśī. There are eight types of such special Dvādaśīs, four that have to do with the length of the tithi, and four  that have to do with lunar asterisms. These Mahā-dvādaśīs  can occur at any time of the year. If Ekādaśī is followed by  Mahā-dvādaśī, one should always fast on Mahā-dvādaśī instead of Ekādaśī.
The first type of Mahā-dvādaśī is Unmīlanī. When Ekādaśī  begins in the proper way (at least two muhūrtas before sunrise) but then continues on beyond the next sunrise, the following day is called Unmīlanī Maha-dvādaśī. Since a tithi may  be up to 26 hours long, this will happen now and then. One  should fast on that day. (The same would of course apply if the  Ekādaśī had begun less than two muhūrtas before the first  sunrise, but in that case the next day would be a normal pure  Ekādaśī, as per the rules above.)
Vañjulī Mahā-dvādaśī is when Ekādaśī is pure, but the  next Dvādaśī begins before sunrise and extends beyond the  next sunrise. In this case one should fast on Dvādaśī. The  Dvādaśī does not have to begin two muhūrtas before sunrise.
Trisprśa Mahā-dvādaśī again is when Ekādaśī begins at  least two muhūrtas before sunrise, but Dvādaśī is so short that  before the next sunrise the next tithi, Trayodaśī, has already  begun. One should fast on that day and break the fast on the  next day, which will be Trayodaśī.
When the new moon or full moon tithi begins before sunrise and continues beyond the next sunrise, the preceding Dvādaśī is called Paksavardhinī Mahā-dvādaśī.

When the Punarvasu, Sravana, Rohinī or Pusya-asterisms  are combined with Dvādaśī in the bright fortnight after sunrise, we have Jayā, Vijayā, Jayantī or Pāpanāśinī Mahā-dvādaśī, respectively. If the moon enters these asterisms on Dvādaśī after sunrise, one should fast on that day, whether the  asterism lasts longer than, as long as, or a shorter time than  the tithi. If the sun enters the asterism before sunrise on Dvādaśī, one should fast on that day, if the asterism lasts longer  than or as long as the tithi. Furthermore, for the day to be a  Mahā-dvādaśī, the Dvādaśī tithi must last until sunset, except if the asterism is Sravana.
So that one does not transgress the Dvādaśī, one should  break the fast after the end of the asterism, if the tithi is longer  than the asterism, except if the constellation is Rohinī or  Sravana. In that case, the fast should be broken in the middle  of the asterism. If the tithi is shorter, the fast should be broken  in the middle of the tithi.
SPECIAL EKADAŚIS AND DVADAŚIS
There are also special festivals at different times of the year  which have particular rules for worship. Different Purānas  contain descriptions of a great amount of these, and the author  has selected those which are mandatory for Vaisnavas. They  all occur on Ekādaśī or Dvādaśī during the bright fortnight,  śukla-paksa. Most festivals are to be celebrated on Dvādaśī,  but if the Dvādaśī is not a Mahā-dvādaśī, the fasting should be  done in the usual way on Ekādaśī.
Bhaimī Ekādaśī comes in the month of Māgha. Contrary  to the others, following special rules on this festival is not mandatory for Vaisnavas. Amardakī Ekādaśī is in Phālguna. If the  following Dvādaśī is combined with the asterism Pusya, it is  called Govinda Dvādaśī, and the festival and fasting is celebrated on that day. Damanāropana Dvādaśī is in Caitra, but  if there is any obstacle to performing the festival at that time,  in Vaiśākhā or Sravana. Nirjala Ekādaśī ís when the sun is
situated in Taurus or in Gemini in the month of Jyaistha. Pavitrāropana Dvādaśī is in Srāvana. In an emergency it can be  done on any bright fortnight when the sun is in the Cancer,  Leo or Virgo, but never when the sun is in Libra.
If Ekādaśī or Dvādaśī in the bright fortnight of Bhadra is  connected with the asterism Śravana, it is called Śravana Ekādaśī or Dvādaśī and has some special rituals connected with it  since it is the appearance day of Lord Vāmana. One should fast  on the day combined with Sravana. If the tithi is so short that  (pure) Ekādaśī and Dvādaśī, both connected with Śravana,  occur on the same day, it is called Visnu-śrńkhala, the chain of  Visnu. Fasting on this day is very meritorious. If both Ekādaśī  and Dvādaśī are connected with Śravana but on different days,  one may exceptionally fast on both if one is able, or else only  on Dvādaśī. If neither is combined with Śravana, one should  fast on Ekādaśī and worship Vāmana on Dvādaśī.
The most important festivals are Śayana, Pārśva-parivartana and Prabodhanī Dvādaśī, when Visnu goes to sleep  before the rainy period, turns over in His sleep and wakes up.  The first is celebrated on the bright Dvādaśī in Asādha, the second likewise in Bhadra, and the third in Kārttika. If the asterism  Revatī is present at night on Prabodhanī Dvādaśī, Visnu should  be awakened in the afternoon. If Revatī is present in the beginning of the day or not at all, Visnu should be awakened at dusk.
APPENDIX II
JĪVA GOSVĀMĪ ON EKĀDAŚĪ
In the Bhakti-sandarbha, in the context of describing the process of arcana or ceremonial worship of the Lord, Jīva Gosvāmī  also discusses Ekādaśī. Passage 299 begins with a description  of Janmāstamī, and then continues like this:
Ekādaśī is certainly mandatory even for non-Vaisnavas. The  general rule is given in the Visnu-dharma: A Vaisnava or Saura  should perform the Ekādaśī vrata', and in the Saura Purāna:  `This a Vaisnava, Saura or Saiva should fully celebrate.` The  Nārada-pañcāratra, while describing the necessary duties after initiation, gives the following specifics in the passage beginning with `I will tell you the rules': `One should not eat on  Ekādaśī of both fortnights. At night one should keep vigil and  especially worship the Almighty.'
About this subject the Visnu-yāmala says:`To differentiate  between the dark and light fortnight, to engage in sinful activities, to eat fruits or other things even though healthy and to  perform the Śrāddha-ceremony: these things are forbidden on  the day of Ekādaśī, and on Dvādaśī sleeping in the day-time  and picking tulasī leaves: Bathing Visnu during the day is at  that time also prohibited. While describing Vaisnava-dharma  the Padma Purāna, Uttara-khanda, states: `Dvādaśī should be  performed with firm faith:
In the Skanda Purāna, Kāśī-khanda, and in the Garuda Purāna, the greatness of Dvārakā, Candraśarman says this about  Bhagavad-dharma:`O Krsna, listen to what I will do from this  day on. I will not eat on Ekādaśī. I will always perform the  vigil. Daily will I worship You here with great devotion. When  your day is not touched by even half a moment of Daśamī it  can bestow liberation. I will also perform the eight Dvādaśīs  that please You: And so forth.
It is said in the Agni Purāna: `On Ekādaśī one should not  eat. This is a great Vaisnava vrata.' In the Gautamīya-tantra: `If  a Vaisnava by madness eats on Ekādaśī, then his worship of  Visnu is worthless. He attains a terrible hell.` In the Matsyaand Bhavisya Purānas: `One who fasts on Ekādaśī should eat  on Dvādaśī. Whether during the light or dark forthnight, this  is a great Vaisnava vrata: In the Skanda Purāna: `One who eats  on Ekādaśī is a killer of his mother, father, brother and guru.  He will fall from Vaikuntha.'
That Vaisnavas should not eat indeed means that they  should avoid even Mahā-prasāda, since generally eating anything else is forbidden for them. As it is said in the Nāradapañcāratra: `O Nārada, one should always accept prasāda,  but not on Ekādaśī. This pertains to devotees of Rāma and  so forth, what to speak of others: In the Brahmānda Purāna:  `One should not enjoy a leaf, a flower, a fruit, water, food,  drink and medicine that has not been offered. This is the prescription for eating. Those men who eat unoffered food make  themselves eligible for atonement. Therefore, always offer  everything to Visnu and then enjoy.'
The vigil is mandatory, as stated in the Skanda Purāna, in a  discussion between Umā and Maheśvara: `The fortune of one  who does not keep vigil on the night of Visnu is destroyed just  like by insulting Vaisnavas. He will not have any interest in the  vigil of Dvādaśī, and he will not have eligibility for worshipping Keśava:
That this vrata is pleasing to Visnu we hear in the Padma  Purāna, Uttara-khanda:`Listen, goddess, as I will explain the  regulations for Dvādaśī. By even a moment's remembrance  of it, Janārdana becomes most pleased: In the Bhavisya Purāna: `Ekādaśī is greatly meritorious, a dispeller of all sins, a  lamp illuminating devotion to Visnu and a bestower of the  highest goal:
Therefore, since this vrata is demonstrated in the Bhāgavata  even by persons firmly situated in devotion, such as Srīmad  Ambarīsa, who only eat Mahā-prasāda, it is understood to be
an intrinsic part of Vaisnava-dharma. This is the conclusion.  Also, in the Padma Purāna, the greatness of Kārttika, we hear  that through the power of the Ekādaśī vrata in the month of  Kārttika, a brāhmana girl attained the position of someone  very dear to the Lord, Satyābhāmā. What more can one say?
APPENDIX III
VEDIC MANTRAS  CITED IN THE TEXT
In the text of the Hari-bhakti-vilāsa translated above, two Vedic  mantras have been referred to, the first for establishing the  Lord (e.g. 13.515), and the second for awakening the Lord (16.  327). So that persons without the qualification for chanting  such mantras would not accidentally recite them, the author  has not given them in full. I have included them here for the  sake of completeness.
It must however be stressed that according to orthodox  Vaisnavas, women and śūdras are not allowed to chant mantras  from the Śruti portion of the Vedic literature. Baladeva Vidyābhūsana holds the same view in his Govinda-bhāsya commentary to Vedānta-sūtra 1.3.34-38. This applies also to those twiceborn men who simply do not know how to chant such mantras.  As can be seen from the story of Vrtrāsura in the Bhāgavatam  (6.9-I2), a small mistake in chanting such mantras can lead to  unwanted results.

APPENDIX V


The Sarvatobhadra mandala should be drawn on the floor using either rice flour and powder from different kinds of ground  beans, or then with paint. One should begin by drawing the  grid and then work from the inside out. Care should be taken  to insure that one side is to the exact North. The dimensions  of the mandala are flexible, as long as the internal proportions  are not changed. After this the main gods should be invoked  into the mandala, since it represents the universe. The following ís a simplified procedure in accordance with the Dig-darśinītīkā to verse 19.905 of the Hari-bhakti-vilāsa.
This formula should be used for invoking each god or group  of gods: o¥ bhūr bhuvah svah/ (Name of the god in the vocative  case) ihāgaccha!/ (Name of the god in the dative case) namah/  (Name of the god in the accusative case) avāhāyami (showing the  mudra for invocation), sthāpayāmi (showing the mudra for establishing).
For example: o¥ bhūr bhuvah svah/brahman ihāgaccha!/brahmane namah/brahmānam avahāyāmi, sthāpayāmi.`O¥ bhūr bhuvah svah. Come here, Brahmā! Obeisance to Brahmā. Brahmā I  invoke, establish:
The god or group of gods should then be worshipped with  sandalwood pulp, flowers and aksata. The following is the order  in which the gods should be invoked and established, their place  in the mandala and the vocative, dative and accusative cases of  their names:

Before disassembling the Mandala, the divinities present in it  should be asked to depart. After clapping the hands three times,  one should utter the following words while showing the āvāhanamudra: o¥ name of the god in the vocative case) sva-sthānam  gaccha/om bhūr bhuvah svah.

GLOSSARY  ACAMANA - a ritual of purification by sipping water  and uttering mantras.
ACYUTA -`The infallible oné, name of Visnu or Krsna.  AGAMAS - a class of scriptures.
AGNIHOTRA - the daily fire sacrifice of brāhmanas.  AKSATA - unhusked barley-corn.
ANANTA -`Unlimited one; name of Visnu or Krsna.  APSARA - a heavenly nymph. . ,
ĀRĀTRIKA - a ceremony of offering things such as incense  and lamps in circles in front of the form of the Lord.  ARGHYA - a ritual gift to the Lord, consisting mainly of a
coconut and a conchshell filled with water.
AŚRAMA - stage of life. Consists of the stage of a brahmacārī,  grhastha, vanaprastha and sannyāsī.
AVATĀRA - divine descent.
BHĀGAVATA - another name for a Vaisnava.
BHĪMA - one of the Pāndavas, heroes of the Mahābhārata.  BHĪMASENA - another name of Bhīma.
BRAHMA - one of the main gods of Hinduism, the creator.  BRAHMACĀRĪ - a celibate student, the first stage of life.  BRAHMAN - God, especially His impersonal aspect.
BRĀHMANA - the priestly or intellectual class of men.  BRAHMA-RĀKSASA - a type of especially malicious ghost.  CAITANYA - Bengali reformer and saint (1486-1534).
Considered a combined avatāra of Rādhā and Krsna by  his followers.
CAKRA - the disk weapon of Visnu.  CANDRA - the moon god.
DAITYA - descendant of Diti, demon.  DANAVA - descendant of Danu, demon.
DAŚAMI - the tenth tithi of the bright and dark fortnight.  Must not be mixed with Ekādaśī.
DAŚARATHA - the father of Rāma.  DEVAKĪ - the mother of Krsna.  DEVĪ - the consort of Śiva.
DHARMA - natural law, proper conduct, duty, religion.  DHARMA-RĀJA -`King of dharma; name of Yama.
DHRUVA - a prince who became a saint and who has his own  Vaikuntha-world.
DHRUVA-LOKA - the pole star, the Vaikuntha-world where  Dhruva dwells.
DOLAYĀTRĀ - the festival of placing the form of Krsna on a  swing.
DURGA - name of Devī.
DVĀDAŚĪ - the twelfth tithi of the bright and dark fortnight.  Day of breaking the fast or sometimes of fasting.  EKĀDAŚĪ - the eleventh tithi of the bright and dark  fortnight. Day of fasting.
GANDHARVAS - a type of heavenly beings.  GARUDA - the golden bird carrier of Visnu.
GAUDĪYA VAISNAVAS - a branch of Vaisnavas who follow  the teachings.of Caitanya.
GAVYA - products of the cow, such as milk.
GAYĀ - a famous city in modern Bihar, place of pilgrimage  and Śrāddha.
GITA - the Bhagavad-gītā, one of the most popular scriptures of Hinduism.
GOPALA -`The cowherd', name of Krsna.
GOPĪCANDANA - a type of yellow clay widely used for tilaka.  GOROCANA - a yellow dye prepared from the bile of cattle.  GOVINDA -`Lord of the cowś, name of Krsna.
GRHASTHA - householder, the second of the four stages of life.  HARI - name of Visnu or Krsna.
I-IEAVaN - a temporary abode of enjoyment, a reward for a  pious life.
HEMĀDRI - authoritative author on dharma, most known  for his work Catur-varga-cintāmani. I3`th century.
HOMA - a sacrifice of ghee and grains into a sacred fire.  ITIHĀSA - the epic scriptures Rāmāyana and Mahābhārata.  INDRA - the king of heaven.
JAGANNĀTHA -`Lord of the universé, name of Visnu or  Krsna.
JAMADAGNI - father of Krsnás descent Paraśurāma.  JANĀRDANA -`Exciter of meń, name of Visnu or Krsna.  JAPA - the silent muttering of mantras.
JAYA -`Conquer!', a joyous exclamation.
KALI - I) a demon, 2) the present, dark age of quarrel with a  Length of 432 00o years.
KALPA - a day of Brahmā, 4 320 000 00O solar years.  KA¥SA - a great demon killed by Krsna.
KAVACA - a protective shield constructed with mantras and  mudrās.
KEŚAVA -`Master of the senses', name of Visnu or Krsna.  KĪRTANA - glorification, generally to sing the glories of God  accompanied by musical instruments and dance.
KRSNA -`The all-attractive oné, the highest God according  to Gaudīya Vaisnavas, origin even of Visnu.
KRSNADEVA - writer on dharma, most known for the work  Prayoga-sāra.
KSATRIYA - the martial or administrative class of men.  KUMBHA - a sacred pot actually consisting of two pots on  top of each others into which the Lord is called for worship.
KU¥KUMA - a red powder used as a cosmetic and in rituals.  KURUKSETRA - a place of pilgrimage in northern modern  Uttar Pradesh and site of the Mahābhārata war.

LAKSMĪ - the goddess of fortune, consort of Visnu.  LIŃGA - the phallic form of Śiva.
MADHU - a great demon killed by Visnu.
MĀDHAVA -`Descendant of the mooń, name of Visnu or  Krsna.
MADHUSŪDANA -`Killer of the Madhu-demon', name of  Visnu or Krsna.
MAHĀDEVĪ -`Great goddess', a name of Devī.  MAHĀ-DVĀDAŚĪ - a special Dvādaśī when fasting is enjoined.  MAHA-PRASADA - the remnants of food that has been  offered to the Lord
MAHEŚVARA -`Great lord', name of Siva.
MANDALA - a sacred geometrical diagram generally drawn  on the floor. Widespread in the tantric tradition.  MANDAPA - a pavillion.
MANTRA - a prayer or an independently powerful word or  phrase.
MANVANTARA - I/14 of a kalpa, or about 308 57I 429 solar  years.
MATHURĀ - city in modern Uttar Pradesh of India where  Krsna was born on earth.
MUDRĀ - a special position of the hands used during  ceremonies.
MUKUNDA - `Giver of liberation; name of Visnu or Krsna.  MUNI - sage.
NĀRADA - sage amongst the gods, son of Brahmā.  NĀRĀYANA -Abode of men', name of Visnu.
NAVAMI - the ninth tithi of the bright and dark fortnight.  NYĀSA -`placing, a generally tantric ritual of spiritualising  the body or an object where the Lord is called to enter by  uttering mantras and touching with the fingers.
om - mystic syllable that begins most mantras. Also called  Pranaya.
PADMANĀBHA -`Lotus-naveled one, name of Visnu.  PAÑCARĀTRAS - type of scripture mainly dealing with  rituals and Vaisnava theology.
PAŚUPĀTAS - type of Saiva scripture.
PRAHLĀDA - king of the demons, but not a demon himself  but a devotee of Visnu.
PRAYĀGA - the confluence of the rivers Gańgā, Yamunā and  (invisible) Sarasvatī. Part of present-day Allahabad.  PURĀNAS - a class of popular religious scriptures dealing for
example with philosophy, ritual and mythology.  RĀDHĀ - the consort of Krsna.
RAMĀ - another name for Laksmī.
RĀMA - an avatāra of Krsna, the hero of the epic Rāmāyana.  RĀMACANDRA - another name for Rāma.
RASA - aesthetic taste or mellow.
RĀSA-DANCE - the mystical circular dance enacted by  Krsna and the cowherdesses in Vrndāvana.  RASĀTALA - a wonderful subterranean world.  RĀSIKA - an expert taster of rasa, connoisseur.  ROOT-MANTRA - a mantra given by the guru.
RSI - seer, wise man.
RUKMANGADA - son of Salya, the king of Madras.
ŚAKTl - I) female divine principle and energy, 2) name of Devī,  3) name of Laksmī.
ŚAIVA - devotee of Siva.
ŚĀLAGRĀMA - ammonite, a special black stone found in the  Gandhakī river basin in Nepal, which often has fossile  marks and which is considered identical with Visnu.
SANDHYA-VANDANA - observance at sunrise, midday and  sunrise by ācamana, worship and japa.
ŚAŃKARA -Auspicious one', name of Śiva.  SANNYĀSĪ - renunciate, the fourth stage of life.  ŚĀSTRA - scripture.
SAURA - devotee of the sun god.
SEEDMANTRA - a mantra consisting of only one mystically  powerful syllable.
SEVEN ISLES - the isles of the world.  SIDDHAS - a class of perfected divine beings.  ŚIVA - one of the main gods of Hinduism.  SMĀRTA - follower of the Smrti. Usually monistic.  SMRTI - class of scriptures dealing with dharma and law.  ŚRADDHA - ritual offering to a departed ancestor.  ŚRAUTA - pertaining to the Śruti.
ŚRĪ - honorific title.
ŚRUTI - the `heard' portion of the Vedic literature,  consisting of the Sa¥hitās, Aranyakas, Brāhmanas and  Upanisads. Considered authoritative by all Hindus.
SUDARŚANA -`Beautiful, the disk weapon of Visnu.  ŚŪDRA - the labourer class of men.
ŚVETADVIPA - `The white isle', a name of heaven.  TILAKA - sectarian mark on the forehead generally made  of clay.
TITHI - lunar day. See appendix I.
TĪRTHA - place of holy bath and pilgrimage.  TRAYODAŚĪ - the thirteenth tithi of the bright and dark  fortnight. Day of breaking the fast when fasting on Dvādaśī.
TRIVIKRAMA -`Three-strider', name of Visnu.  TULASĪ - holy basil (Occimum Sanctum), a shrub dear to  Visnu and sacred to Vaisnavas. Krsna is said not to accept  any food offered without a tulasī leaf.
TWICE-BORN - a member of the three higher castes  (brāhmanas, ksatriyas and vaiśyas), but often simply  another name for a brāhmana.
UMĀ - a name of Devī.
VAIKUNTHA - Visnu's eternal abode.
VAISNAVA - devotee of Visnu or one of His forms such as  Rāma or Krsna.
VAIŚYA - the mercantile or agricultural class of men.  VAJRA - the weapon of Indra, usually concieved of as a  thunderbolt.
VANAPRASTHA - the retired, reclusive stage of life.  VARNA - class of men. The four classes are brāhmana,  ksatriya, vaiśya and śūdra.
VASUS - a class of heavenly beings.
VEDA - basic four scriptures of Hinduism. Also a general  term for all Hindu scripture.
VEDANGA - auxiliary parts of the Uedas, dealing with ritual,  grammar and so on.
VENKATA ĀCĀRYA - original name of Vedānta Deśika, a  very influential Srī Vaisnava theologian. 14th century.  VILĀSA -'manifestation', chapter of the Hari-bhakti-vilāsa.  VISNU - one of the main gods of Hinduism. According to
Gaudīya Vaisnavas, an aspect of Krsna.
VISNUMAYĪ =`The energy of Visnú, a name of Laksmī.  VISNU-SAHASRA-NĀMA - a hymn of the Mahābhārata  containing one thousand names of Visnu.
VOPADEVA - author of many works on dharma, grammar and  other subjects. I3th century.
VRATA - a ritual vow or observance. See introduction.  YAKSAS - a class of semi-divine beings.
YAMA - the god of death. Also known as Yamarāja.  YANTRA - a mystical diagram with mantras, usually  inscribed upon a piece of metal.
YOJANA - a distance of about eleven kilometres.  YUGA - age of the world, with a length of 1 728 000,  I 296 000, 864 000 or 432 00O years.

ABOUT THE AUTHOR  Bhrgumuni Dāsa (Máns Broo) was born in Ábo, Finland 1973.  He joined ISKCON in 199a and received initiation from Śrīpād  Suhotra Swami in 1996. Since then, he has been pursuing academic studies at Abo Akademi University (Comparative Religion) and Uppsala University (Indology). He received his MA  in 1999.
He lives with his wife Laura in Helsinki, Finland, where he  is currently working on his Ph.D. about the role of the guru  within the Gaudiya Vaisnava movement. He has been an enthusiastic follower of Ekādaśī for several years, so the present  book has very much been a labour of love.
